Source: ABI and Syscalls - SoftSec Lab @ KAIST
URL: https://softsec.kaist.ac.kr/depot/sangkilc/is50601/04-abi.pdf
Lec 4: ABI and Syscalls IS50601: Binary Code Analysis and Secure Software Systems Sang Kil Cha
Application Binary Interface
Interface? An interface is a shared boundary across which two or more components of a system exchange information1
1https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interface_(computing)
Interface? An interface is a shared boundary across which two or more components of a system exchange information1  1https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interface_(computing)
Application Programming Interface (API) A shared boundary across two or more computer programs. Library implementation Linux TensorFlow B2R2 etc. Program API
Application Binary Interface (ABI) A shared boundary between binaries. Binary (Library, OS, etc.) Linux TensorFlow B2R2 etc. Program binary ABI
ABI Details ISA. Data types (e.g., size of integer/float types, endianness) Calling convention (e.g., register use). System call convention. Binary format (e.g., ELF).
Calling Conventions Calling convention dictates how a function call is implemented at the assembly level. How parameters are passed (i.e., through registers or stack), in which order. Which registers are preserved across function calls.
Calling Conventions Calling convention dictates how a function call is implemented at the assembly level. How parameters are passed (i.e., through registers or stack), in which order. Which registers are preserved across function calls. Why would you preserve register values across function calls?
Volatile vs. Non-Volatile Registers Volatile registers are not necessarily preserved across function calls. - So the caller needs to save the value if it is necessary (caller-saved registers). Non-volatile registers are preserved across function calls. - The callee is responsible for preserving the value (callee-saved registers).
MIPS Calling Convention First four arguments are passed through a0–a3. The fifth and subsequent arguments are passed through the stack. The return value is passed through $v0. Volatile registers: t0–t9. Non-volatile registers: s0–s7.
x86 Linux Calling Convention Often referred to as cdecl. Push arguments to the stack in reverse order. The return value is passed through eax (or ST0 for a FP value). Volatile registers: eax, ecx, and edx. foo(1, 2, 3); push 3 push 2 push 1 call foo add esp, 12
x86-64 Linux Calling Convention First six arguments are passed through rdi, rsi, rdx, rcx, r8, and r9. The seventh and subsequent arguments are passed through the stack. The return value is passed through rax (or ST0 for a FP value). Volatile registers: rax, rcx, rdx, r8, r9, r10, r11. foo(1, 2, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7); push 7 mov r9, 6 mov r8, 5 mov rcx, 4 mov rdx, 3 mov rsi, 2 mov rdi, 1 call foo
Question Do you think every function in a binary should strictly follow the ABI? Why or why not?
System Calls
Syscall = System Call A system call is a request to the kernel to perform a privileged operation. System call convention is a part of OS ABI. We can invoke a system call by executing a special instruction.
int 0x80 on x86 Linux. - syscall on x86-64 and MIPS Linux. - svc on ARM Linux. - ecall on RISC-V Linux.
Linux Syscall Convention ISA Instr. Num. Arguments Return x86 int 0x80 eax ebx, ecx, edx, esi, edi, ebp eax x86-64 syscall rax rdi, rsi, rdx, r10, r8, r9 rax See: man syscall.
Syscall Numbers /usr/include/i386-linux-gnu/asm/unistd_32.h
define __NR_restart_syscall 0 # define __NR_exit 1 # define __NR_fork 2 # define __NR_read 3 # define __NR_write 4 # define __NR_open 5 # define __NR_close 6 # define __NR_waitpid 7 # define __NR_creat 8 # define __NR_link 9 # define __NR_unlink 10 # define __NR_execve 11 ...
Syscalls Have a Wrapper Function void _exit(int status ); pid_t fork(void ); ssize_t read(int fd , void *buf , size_t count ); ssize_t write(int fd , const void *buf , size_t count ); int open(const char *pathname , int flags , mode_t mode ); ... These are defined in the C library (libc), and they internally invoke a syscall.
Writing a Hello World Program in Assembly C Hello World #include <stdio.h> int main(void) { puts("hello world"); return 0; } Several problems: Who is going to invoke main? How do we terminate the program after returning from main? puts is an external function defined in libc.
Learning from Compiler (GCC) Compile the C program with the following command: gcc -S -masm=intel -o hello.s hello.c And observe the generated assembly code (hello.s).
Simplified Assembly Code2 .intel_syntax noprefix .text .section .rodata .LC0: .string " hello world " .text .globl main main: push rbp mov rbp , rsp lea rax , [rip + .LC0] mov rdi , rax call puts@PLT mov eax , 0 pop rbp ret The problem still remains: Who is going to invoke main? How do we terminate the program after returning from main? puts is an external function defined in libc.
2You may see some differences in the generated code depending on the compiler version used. Plus, GCC uses slightly different Intel syntax than the one defined by Intel, so I changed it.
Simplified Assembly Code2 .intel_syntax noprefix .text .section .rodata .LC0: .string " hello world " .text .globl main main: push rbp mov rbp , rsp lea rax , [rip + .LC0] mov rdi , rax call puts@PLT mov eax , 0 pop rbp ret The problem still remains: Who is going to invoke main? How do we terminate the program after returning from main? puts is an external function defined in libc. Indeed, this is just an assembly version of the program using the C API. 2You may see some differences in the generated code depending on the compiler version used. Plus, GCC uses slightly different Intel syntax than the one defined by Intel, so I changed it.
Who Invokes main? Compile hello.s with the following command: gcc -o hello hello.s And disassemble the resulting binary file (hello) to figure out the followings. The entry point of the binary is _start, not main. The _start function will simply invoke __libc_start_main, but nothing else. The main function is never invoked in the binary code.
main is invoked by the C runtime (crt0). - The C runtime is a part of the C library (libc). - The C runtime is invoked by the kernel.
Writing the Program Purely with Linux ABI Use the write syscall to print the string. - You can write to the standard output by passing 1 as the first argument3. Use the exit syscall to terminate the program. 3stdin = 0, stdout = 1, stderr = 2.
Boilerplate Code x86 .intel_syntax noprefix .text .globl _start _start : mov eax , 1 int 0x80 x86-64 .intel_syntax noprefix .text .globl _start _start : mov rax , 60 syscall
Shellcoding
Shellcode A small piece of code that invokes a “shell”, i.e., /bin/sh.
Shellcode in C #include <stdio.h> void main(void) { char* argv [] = { "/bin/sh", NULL }; execve("/bin/sh", argv , NULL ); } execve executes the program specified by the first argument (/bin/sh) with the arguments specified by the second argument (argv) and the environment variables specified by the third argument (NULL).
execve is a System Call int execve(const char *pathname , char *const argv [], char *const envp []); Therefore, we can write a shellcode that invokes execve directly.
Shellcode in Assembly (x86) .intel_syntax noprefix .text .globl _start _start : mov eax , 11 lea ebx , .shell lea ecx , .argv mov edx , 0 int 0x80 .shell : .string "/bin/sh" .argv: .long .shell , 0
Disassembling Our Shellcode b2r2 dump <file> 401000: B8 0B 00 00 00 mov EAX , 0xb 401005: 8D 1C 25 1A 10 40 00 lea EBX , dword ptr [0 x40101a ] 40100 c: 8D 0C 25 22 10 40 00 lea ECX , dword ptr [0 x401022 ] 401013: BA 00 00 00 00 mov EDX , 0x0 401018: CD 80 int 0x80 40101 a: 2F ( illegal ) 40101 b: 62 ( illegal ) 40101 c: 69 6E 2F 73 68 00 1A imul EBP , dword ptr [RSI +0 x2f], 0 x1a006873 401023: 10 40 00 adc byte ptr [RAX +0 x0], AL 401026: 00 00 add byte ptr [RAX], AL 401028: 00 00 add byte ptr [RAX], AL We say this is not a valid shellcode. Why?
Critical Shellcode Requirement It should run regardless of the address it is loaded. In other words, it should be position independent. (Optional) Sometimes, we want to make our shellcode null-free, or similarly, we want to impose some other constraints on the shellcode, depending on the context.
Making a Position-Independent Shellcode Two problems: 1. The address of the string /bin/sh is hardcoded in the code. 2. The address of the string /bin/sh is hardcoded in the .argv data. The first problem is easily solved in x86-64 because we can simply use the PC-relative addressing mode: [rip + <offset>]. However, for x86, we cannot use such a form of operand. The second problem cannot be directly solved in either x86 or x86-64. So we have to dynamically create an array.
PC-relative Addressing in x86-64 lea rdi, .shell lea rdi, [rip + .shell]
Dynamically Createing argv Array Stack is a good place to store temporary data! push 0 ; NULL push <address of /bin/sh > mov rsi , rsp ; rsi points to the array on the stack
Creating a Shellcode for x86-64 .intel_syntax noprefix .text .globl _start _start : mov rax , 59 lea rdi , [rip+ .shell ] push 0 push rdi mov rsi , rsp mov rdx , 0 syscall .shell : .string "/bin/sh"
Test Position-Independence Take the bytes of the shellcode and put them in a file. Then feed in the file as input to the following program.
include < stdio .h> # include <unistd .h> # include <sys/mman.h> void main( void ) {
char buf [512]; read (0, buf , sizeof (buf )); mprotect (( void )(( long )buf / 4096 * 4096) ,4096 , PROT_READ | PROT_WRITE | PROT_EXEC ); (( void (*)()) buf )(); }
Question?
Exercise The x86-64 shellcode we created contains null bytes. Create a null-free version of the shellcode. Make your own x86 shellcode that is position-independent. Optionally, you could make it null-free, too. Try to make your shellcode as small as possible.

This part covers how exceptions are taken and returned, the structure of the VBAR_EL1 vector table, GIC interrupt routing, ESR_EL1 syndrome decoding, and practical fault-debugging techniques in assembly. Table of Contents × Introduction & Privilege Model Exception Levels EL0–EL3 EL0 (User) & EL1 (Kernel) EL2 (Hypervisor) & EL3 (Secure Monitor) SPSR_ELn & ELR_ELn Exception Types Synchronous Exceptions (SVC/HVC/SMC) IRQ / FIQ / SError ESR_EL1 Syndrome Decoding EC Quick Reference Table AArch32 Support at Exception Levels Vector Table (VBAR_EL1) Layout & Entry Offsets Writing a Vector Table in Assembly GIC — Generic Interrupt Controller Distributor & CPU Interface GIC Initialisation Assembly Exception Entry & Return Automatic Save on Entry ERET & Level Transitions Fault Debugging Techniques Hands-On Exercises Exception Plan Generator Conclusion & Next Steps Introduction & Privilege Model Series Overview: This is Part 11 of our 28-part ARM Assembly Mastery Series. Parts 1–10 covered architecture through floating-point. Now we enter system-level programming — the exception model that underpins every OS, hypervisor, and firmware stack built on ARM hardware. confidential compute, AI accel AArch64 defines four exception levels — EL0 through EL3 — that map directly to privilege rings in modern operating systems. EL0 is unprivileged user space; EL1 is the OS kernel; EL2 is the hypervisor; EL3 is the secure monitor (firmware). Each level has its own dedicated system registers (SP_EL0/SP_EL1/SP_EL2/SP_EL3, SPSR_EL1/EL2/EL3, ELR_EL1/EL2/EL3) and can only be entered via an exception — never by a normal branch. Real-World Analogy — The Building Security Model: Think of a four-floor secure building. EL0 (Ground Floor) is the public lobby — visitors can walk around freely but can't open any locked doors. EL1 (Office Floor) is like the main office — employees with keycards can access files and meeting rooms but not the server room. EL2 (Building Management) is the facilities centre — the building manager controls HVAC, elevators, and which tenants can access which floors. EL3 (Vault Floor) is the underground vault — only the owner with biometric access can enter, and it stores the master keys that control the entire building. You can only move between floors via a controlled elevator (the exception mechanism) — never by climbing through the ceiling. Exception Levels EL0–EL3 EL0 (User) & EL1 (Kernel) EL0 is the least privileged level. User applications run here and cannot directly access system registers or execute privileged instructions. Attempting to do so raises a Synchronous exception that is taken to EL1. EL1 is the OS kernel level — it controls virtual memory (through TTBR0_EL1/TTBR1_EL1), manages interrupt masking (DAIF), and handles the vector table (VBAR_EL1). The kernel services user requests via the SVC (Supervisor Call) instruction. AArch64 exception level hierarchy — EL0 (user), EL1 (kernel), EL2 (hypervisor), EL3 (secure monitor) ARM Exception Level Hierarchy Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light EL2 (Hypervisor) & EL3 (Secure Monitor) EL2 is the hypervisor level — it intercepts guest OS operations via HVC (Hypervisor Call) and implements stage-2 address translation (VTTBR_EL2) to isolate virtual machines. EL2 is optional in implementations without the Virtualization Extensions. EL3 is the highest privilege and is always Secure — it hosts Trusted Firmware-A (TF-A), handles the Secure Monitor Call (SMC) instruction, and performs world switches between Secure and Non-Secure states via the SCR_EL3.NS bit. Secure vs Non-Secure Worlds ARM TrustZone divides the entire system into two isolated worlds, orthogonal to exception levels. The Secure world (S-EL0, S-EL1, EL3) runs trusted firmware, cryptographic key stores, and DRM stacks. The Non-Secure world (NS-EL0, NS-EL1, NS-EL2) runs the rich OS and applications. The SCR_EL3.NS bit determines the current security state — when NS=0, the processor addresses Secure memory; when NS=1, Non-Secure memory. A world switch requires an SMC call to EL3, which saves the entire context of one world and restores the other. Memory controllers enforce physical isolation: Non-Secure transactions cannot access Secure-tagged DRAM regions (TZASC/TZC-400), even via DMA. This hardware partitioning protects fingerprint sensors, payment credentials, and boot keys from compromised operating systems. Case Study ARM TrustZone in Mobile Payments Every Android phone with a fingerprint sensor or NFC payment chip uses TrustZone. When you tap to pay with Google Pay, the payment token is generated inside a Trusted Application (TA) running at S-EL0 within a Trusted Execution Environment (TEE) like OP-TEE or Trusty. The TA never exposes the private key to the rich OS — even a fully rooted Android device cannot read Secure world memory. Samsung Knox, Qualcomm's QTEE, and Apple's Secure Enclave all leverage this hardware isolation. The 2019 Qualcomm QSEE vulnerability (CVE-2019-10574) demonstrated that even a vulnerability in the Secure world required sophisticated multi-stage exploitation because the attacker had to first escape NS-EL1 privilege to reach EL3. SPSR_ELn & ELR_ELn Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light Exception Types Synchronous Exceptions (SVC / HVC / SMC) Synchronous exceptions are caused directly by instruction execution: SVC (system call from EL0→EL1), HVC (hypervisor call from EL1→EL2), SMC (secure monitor call to EL3), data aborts, instruction aborts, SP/PC alignment faults, and undefined instruction faults. The handler can determine the cause by reading ESR_EL1 (Exception Syndrome Register). Synchronous exceptions always target one level higher than the current EL (or the same EL for EL1 self-hosted debugger). ARM exception type taxonomy — synchronous exceptions (SVC/HVC/SMC/aborts) vs asynchronous (IRQ/FIQ/SError) IRQ / FIQ / SError IRQ (Interrupt ReQuest) and FIQ (Fast Interrupt reQuest) are asynchronous exceptions driven by the GIC. FIQ targets EL3 by default and can be used for secure-world interrupts. SError (System Error) is an asynchronous abort — typically triggered by an imprecise data abort on an external bus (e.g., uncorrectable ECC error). The DAIF register bits (D=debug, A=SError, I=IRQ, F=FIQ) mask these exceptions; setting a bit inhibits the corresponding exception type. Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light ESR_EL1 Syndrome Decoding Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light Exception Class (EC) Quick Reference The 6-bit EC field in ESR_ELx identifies why the exception occurred. The most important values you will encounter during kernel and hypervisor development are listed below. Note that many EC values come in AArch32/AArch64 pairs — for example, SVC from AArch32 is EC 0x11, while SVC from AArch64 is EC 0x15. Reference ESR Exception Class Values Key Insight — EC 0x15 in Practice: When a 64-bit Linux userspace program calls write() , glibc executes SVC #0 , which triggers a synchronous exception with EC=0x15 (SVC from AArch64). The kernel's EL1 vector table entry at offset +0x400 (synchronous, from lower EL AArch64) reads ESR_EL1 , extracts bits [31:26], compares against 0x15, and branches to the system call handler. If you see EC=0x11 instead, it means a 32-bit AArch32 process made the SVC — which brings us to the topic of AArch32 compatibility. AArch32 Support at Exception Levels Modern Cortex-A cores vary in their AArch32 (32-bit ARM/Thumb) support at different exception levels. This is architecturally configurable — HCR_EL2.RW and SCR_EL3.RW control whether lower ELs execute in AArch32 or AArch64 state: Table AArch32 Support by Cortex-A Generation Practical Impact: On Cortex-A75/A76, you can still run a 32-bit Linux distribution (AArch32 kernel at EL1, 32-bit apps at EL0). Starting with A710, the kernel must be 64-bit — only EL0 (userspace) can execute AArch32 code, supporting legacy 32-bit Android apps. From A715 onward, AArch32 is gone entirely. If your ESR shows EC=0x0E (Illegal Execution State), it may mean an AArch32 binary tried to execute on a core that no longer supports it at that exception level. Vector Table (VBAR_EL1) Layout & Entry Offsets The AArch64 vector table (pointed to by VBAR_EL1) has 16 entries of 128 bytes (32 instructions) each, organised into four groups of four based on the source EL and stack pointer selection: VBAR_EL1 vector table structure — 16 entries of 128 bytes, organized by source EL and SP selection Table Vector Table Structure From Current EL with SP_EL0 (offset +0x000–+0x180): Synchronous, IRQ, FIQ, SError From Current EL with SP_ELx (offset +0x200–+0x380): Synchronous, IRQ, FIQ, SError From Lower EL (AArch64) (offset +0x400–+0x580): Synchronous, IRQ, FIQ, SError From Lower EL (AArch32) (offset +0x600–+0x780): Synchronous, IRQ, FIQ, SError Writing a Vector Table in Assembly Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light GIC — Generic Interrupt Controller Distributor & CPU Interface The GIC-400/GIC-500/GIC-600 (GICv2/GICv3/GICv4) manages interrupt routing in ARM SoCs. The Distributor (GICD) is a shared component that receives interrupts, applies priority and routing configuration, and forwards them to the correct CPU interface. The CPU Interface (GICC in GICv2, ICC_* system registers in GICv3) is per-core — it presents the highest-priority pending interrupt and receives EOI (End Of Interrupt) acknowledgements. GICv3 introduces LPIs (message-based interrupts) and ITS (Interrupt Translation Service) for PCIe MSI. GIC architecture — Distributor routes interrupts to per-core CPU Interfaces which handle acknowledge and EOI GIC Initialisation Assembly Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light Exception Entry & Return Automatic Save on Entry On exception entry AArch64 hardware automatically saves PSTATE into SPSR_ELn and the return address into ELR_ELn. It does NOT push general-purpose registers — the handler is responsible for saving them. A minimal EL1 IRQ handler must save all caller-saved registers (x0–x18, x29, x30) before calling C code. Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light ERET & Level Transitions ERET is the only instruction to return from an exception. It simultaneously loads the PC from ELR_ELn and restores PSTATE from SPSR_ELn, which includes the target EL encoded in SPSR.EL bits. To deliberately transition downward (e.g., drop from EL3 to EL1 at boot), firmware programs SPSR_EL3 with the desired target EL and DAIF mask, sets ELR_EL3 to the entry point, and executes ERET. Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light Fault Debugging Techniques When a data abort occurs, ESR_EL1 ISS field contains the Data Fault Status Code (DFSC) and whether the fault was a read or write (WnR bit). FAR_EL1 (Fault Address Register) holds the virtual address that caused the fault. Combining these three registers — ESR_EL1, ELR_EL1, FAR_EL1 — gives the full picture: what happened, where it happened, and what address was accessed. Fault diagnosis register trio — ESR_EL1 (cause), FAR_EL1 (faulting VA), and ELR_EL1 (faulting instruction PC) Copy Tomorrow Night Default Dark Twilight Okaidia Solarized Light Key Insight: The four exception levels are not a stack — you cannot take nested exceptions across levels automatically. EL1 can only be interrupted by asynchronous exceptions (IRQ/FIQ) if they are configured to target EL1 and DAIF.I=0. Nested EL1 synchronous exceptions (e.g., a data abort inside an exception handler) reuse ELR_EL1 and SPSR_EL1, overwriting the previous values unless the handler saves them explicitly. This is why OS interrupt handlers always save ELR_EL1 and SPSR_EL1 to the kernel stack on entry. History Evolution of ARM Exception Architecture ARM's exception model has evolved dramatically across architecture versions. ARMv4/v5 had seven processor modes (USR, FIQ, IRQ, SVC, ABT, UND, SYS) with banked registers — FIQ had its own R8–R14, giving it extremely low-latency entry. ARMv7-A added Monitor mode for TrustZone and Hyp mode for virtualization, creating a total of nine modes. ARMv8-A replaced the entire mode system with the cleaner four-level EL0–EL3 hierarchy, each with dedicated stack pointers and system registers. This redesign eliminated register banking (which complicated context switching) in favour of explicit save/restore, and decoupled privilege levels from the exception routing mechanism. The transition from ARMv7's "mode-based" to ARMv8's "level-based" model is one of the most significant architectural changes in ARM's 40-year history. GICv3 Interrupt Routing & Affinity: GICv3 introduces affinity-based routing using a 4-level hierarchy (Aff3.Aff2.Aff1.Aff0) that maps to socket.cluster.core.thread. Instead of GICv2's 8-core TARGETS register, GICv3 uses GICD_IROUTER per SPI to route any interrupt to any core in the system. Interrupts are classified as: SGI (Software Generated, IDs 0–15, inter-core signalling), PPI (Private Peripheral, IDs 16–31, per-core timers/PMU), SPI (Shared Peripheral, IDs 32–1019, external devices), and LPI (Locality-specific, IDs 8192+, message-based for PCIe MSI via the ITS — Interrupt Translation Service). FIQ is hardwired to Group 0 (Secure) by default, making it ideal for secure watchdog timers and TEE interrupts. Hands-On Exercises Practice Exercises: Test your understanding of ARM exception levels and interrupt handling. Exercise 1 Vector Table Implementation Task: Write a complete AArch64 vector table that handles all 16 entries. For each entry, save x0–x3 on the stack, call a C function exception_handler(uint64_t type, uint64_t esr, uint64_t elr, uint64_t far) passing the exception type as an enum (0=sync, 1=IRQ, 2=FIQ, 3=SError), restore registers, and ERET. Test by deliberately triggering a data abort (load from address 0x0) and verify your handler prints the correct ESR, ELR, and FAR values via UART. Hint: Use the .org directive to place each handler at the correct 0x80-byte offset. Remember that VBAR_EL1 must be 2KB (0x800) aligned. Exercise 2 GIC Priority Configuration Task: Configure a GICv2 system with three interrupt sources: a UART RX interrupt (SPI 33) at priority 0x40, a timer interrupt (PPI 27) at priority 0x80, and a watchdog interrupt (SPI 34) at priority 0x20 (highest). Verify that when all three fire simultaneously, the watchdog handler runs first, then UART, then timer. Use GICD_ISPENDR to manually pend all three and observe the execution order. Hint: Lower numerical priority values mean higher priority in the GIC. Set GICC_PMR to 0xFF to accept all priorities, then use GICC_IAR reads to confirm the order. Exercise 3 Exception Level Transition Task: Write a firmware stub that boots at EL3, configures SCR_EL3 and HCR_EL2, then drops to EL1 via two ERET transitions (EL3→EL2→EL1). At each level, read CurrentEL and print it via UART to confirm the transition succeeded. Finally, execute an SVC from a simple EL0 task to verify the full round-trip EL0→EL1→EL0. Hint: Program SPSR_ELn with the target EL in bits[3:2] and set ELR_ELn to the entry point of the next stage. Don't forget to set SCR_EL3.RW=1 and HCR_EL2.RW=1 to keep EL1 and EL2 in AArch64 mode. ARMv-8a Exception Levels | Pyjama Cafe! Pyjama Cafe_ Lowering the Barrier to learning Embedded Systems! Library Courses Soan-Papdi Login ARMv-8a Exception Levels By: MbharmalPosted on 9 Jun, 2025 in arm64 • 1759 words • 9 minute read Tags: ARM64, Exception Models ARM-A is the most prevalent architecture out there powering billions of devices. We will be covering these topics over the course of multiple articles in this series. Exception Levels and Security States Hey there! If you're diving into the ARMv8-A architecture you might benefit from this writeup. ARM-A class are a beast of a system, with layers of control and security (sometimes I feel like they are a bit of an overkill). But that's what makes it both powerful and complex. In this one, we will walk through the exception levels (EL0 to EL3) and the security states (Non-secure and Secure). These are the backbone of how ARMv8-A organizes privilege, isolation, and trust. What's ARMv8-A? Before we jump into the nitty-gritty, let's set the stage. ARMv8-A is the architecture behind most modern 64-bit ARM processors (think Cortex-A series, like the ones in your phone or in your latest Mac Mx chip or in server class CPUs). It's designed for flexibility, supporting everything from bare-metal IoT devices to full-blown operating systems like Linux or Android. The key to its power lies in how it manages privilege and security through exception levels and security states. These concepts sort of govern who can access what, when, and how—whether it's an app, an OS kernel, a hypervisor, or a trusted OS or a secure firmware. ARM64 Exception Levels Exception Levels:- ARMv8-A organizes its processing modes into four exception levels, namely - EL0, EL1, EL2, and EL3. Think of these as a stack of privilege, where higher numbers mean more control over the system. Each level runs specific types of software, and the transitions between them are tightly controlled. EL0: The Unprivileged World (User Apps) EL0 is where your everyday applications live—think your browser, games, or that random app you downloaded. It's the least privileged level, meaning it has zero direct access to hardware or sensitive system resources. Code running at EL0 is boxed in by the operating system, which enforces strict boundaries. User applications, like those Android apps or Linux processes run here. No direct hardware access. Everything goes through system calls to higher levels. EL0 code can't even touch the memory management unit (MMU) configuration. The page tables it uses are set up by EL1 (the OS), and EL0 has to trust them blindly. This isolation is great for security as third-party code should seldom be trusted. Below code snippet makes a system call request to EL1 from EL0 (in AArch64 assembly). We take an example of write() system call which we seldom have to bother about given we use a nice wrapper like printf() . The svc instruction triggers an exception, handing control to EL1. The EL0 user space code has no idea how EL1 handles this—it's completely dependent on the OS. EL1: The OS / Kernel Layer EL1 is where the operating system kernel runs, like Linux or Windows. It's got way more power than EL0, with direct access to hardware resources like the MMU, interrupts, and most system registers. This is where the OS manages processes, schedules tasks, and sets up memory mappings. OS kernels, device drivers, and anything that needs low-level system control run here EL1 can configure the system but is still subject to restrictions from higher levels (EL2 or EL3). We will look this in more detail in a bit. For the most part, we can assume EL1 layer has full view of the hardware but it is not a true statement to make for all the use-cases. E.g. Server class applications which have Hypervisor enabled. Please just stay with me for a while, I promise more details are coming in the EL2 section. Code snippet (remember we are only highligting very key details, rest is deliberalety omitted) of EL1 handler for the svc call from EL0: The goal of this code snippet is to demo out the steps involved in handling an svc exception. This is by no means complete but I believe if we weed out the details, we should see some 7-8 instructions that should inspire us that this can be grappled with. But, we will un-cover the details in later artciles. EL2: Hypervisor - (I like to think of it as Scheduler for Kernels/VMs ) EL2 is where hypervisors live—think KVM, VirtualBox or VMware. It's designed for virtualization, allowing a hypervisor to manage multiple guest operating systems (each running at EL1). EL2 has more privilege than EL1, letting it control virtualized hardware and intercept certain operations from guest OSes. Hypervisors or virtual machine monitors (VMMs) run here. They are kernels for kernels (if that makes sense !) EL2 can trap and emulate hardware accesses from EL1, giving it fine-grained control over guest OS behavior. hvc instructions is a synchronous way to request the services of Hypervisor by EL1 layer. EL2's job is to schedule and provide an envrionment where multiple OSes/Kernels can co-exists and run. They provide an isolated view to each of the kernel (making them believe they are the king of the castle). This often means that, they will have to spoof certain transactions or even memory-maps, intercept certain interrupts to achieve this (Again, I will request you to bear with me and wait for these details to be shared in the next articles). This code snippet shows at high level the handling of hvc (Hypervisor call) from EL1 layer (Guest OS or Virtual Machine). EL3: The Secure Monitor EL3 is the most privileged level, reserved for the secure monitor—a piece of firmware that manages transitions between the Secure and Non-secure worlds (more on this in just a bit). It's typically used for trusted boot, or handling sensitive operations like finger-print or face-auth, cryptographic key management, etc. Secure monitor code, like ARM's Trusted Firmware. Switcher of the worlds! smc instruction is a synchronous way to request the services of EL3 Monitor by EL1 or EL2 layers. EL3 has a full and total view and control of the system. The code running here is fully trusted and often supplied by the SoC vendor. This layer is designed and implemented in such a way that it can be assumed that it will be sort of unsurmountable fortress which will be hard to break or bypass. Here's a snippet of EL3 code handling a secure monitor call ( SMC ): Security States: Secure vs. Non-secure Worlds Now, let's talk about the security states: Secure and Non-secure. ARMv8-A splits the system into two worlds to isolate sensitive operations (like handling encryption keys) from regular code (like your OS or apps). This is part of ARM's TrustZone technology. Non-secure world: This is where most software runs—your OS, apps, and even hypervisors (EL0, EL1, EL2). It's “untrusted” in the sense that it can't access Secure world resources. Secure world: This is the trusted side, typically limited to EL3 (secure monitor) and sometimes a secure OS at EL1. It's where sensitive operations happen, like secure boot or trusted applications like finger-print auth, crypto functions, etc. The secure monitor (EL3) acts as a gatekeeper, using the scr_el3 (Secure Configuration Register) to control and switch between the two worlds. The snippet shows that switch from NS to S world. If you've stayed with me so far, I mean kudos to you! What a mouthful was all of that! My request and hope is that if revisited often, this will start making sense and we'd feel comfortable about the architectural design. Please feel free to revisit this if you'd like to torture yourself some bit more :) But, jokes aside, we have covered a lot but still a lot more is to go. We have omitted (deliberately) a lot of details which we will un-cover in the next artciles. We'll dig into the memory management system—page tables, translation regimes, and why cache coherence, interrupt handling and more. I will see you in the next one! The LIBRARY! Source: ARMv8-A Exception Levels Explained: From EL0 to EL3 - Wadix Technologies
URL: https://www.wadixtech.com/blog/armv8-a-exception-levels-el0-to-el3
ARMv8-A Exception Levels Explained: From EL0 to EL3 Home Training Blog Contact us About us Sign in Sign up Home Training Blog Contact us About us Sign in Sign up 17 May, 2026 / Sebastian Helmut Embedded Systems Microcontroller Embedded Security ARMv8-A Exception Levels Explained: From EL0 to EL3 Don't hesitate Get the Professional Embedded Starter Kit: Production-ready templates and architectural cheat sheets for your firmware projects. Get the starter-kit projects now! How ARMv8-A Exception Levels Work: Kernel, Hypervisor, and Secure Monitor
Introduction Every instruction a processor executes runs at a specific privilege level. On ARMv8-A, these levels are called Exception Levels, or ELs. They control what the code running at that level is allowed to do: which registers it can access, which memory it can touch, and which system configuration it can modify. Understanding exception levels is fundamental to understanding how ARMv8-A systems are structured. The separation between user applications, the operating system kernel, a hypervisor, and a secure monitor is not a software convention. It is enforced in hardware by the exception level mechanism. Code running at a lower level simply cannot access the resources of a higher level, regardless of what it tries to do.
What Are Exception Levels 2.1 The Four Levels: EL0, EL1, EL2, EL3 ARMv8-A defines four exception levels numbered EL0 through EL3. The number indicates the privilege level. EL0 is the least privileged and EL3 is the most privileged. EL0: Unprivileged execution. User space applications run here. EL1: Privileged execution. The operating system kernel runs here. EL2: Hypervisor execution. A virtual machine monitor runs here when virtualization is in use. EL3: Secure monitor execution. The firmware or TrustZone monitor runs here. Not all exception levels need to be implemented or active in a given system. EL2 is optional and only meaningful when virtualization is used. EL3 is optional and only meaningful when TrustZone security extensions are used. 2.2 Privilege and Trust Model The exception level model encodes two independent axes: privilege and trust. Privilege controls what system resources are accessible. Code at EL0 cannot configure the MMU, cannot mask interrupts system-wide, and cannot access most system registers. Code at EL1 can do all of these things for the normal world. Code at EL3 has unrestricted access and can configure the entire system including the security state. Trust is orthogonal to privilege. ARMv8-A defines two security states: Secure and Non-Secure. EL3 always executes in the Secure state. EL0 and EL1 can execute in either state depending on how EL3 configures the system. EL2 exists only in the Non-Secure state in most configurations. Fig 1. The four exception levels and their typical software assignments. Struggling to implement this for a professional project? If you need to master full-scale firmware architecture, security, and build automation, join our 1-D or 4-Day Live Implemnetation Workshops. I'll show you the exact direct path to production-ready firmware without the trial and error.What Runs at Each Level 3.1 EL0: User Space Applications EL0 is where user space code runs. Applications, libraries, and user space daemons all execute at EL0. At this level the processor has the minimum privilege. The code cannot directly configure the MMU, cannot access system control registers, and cannot mask exceptions globally. When a user space application needs a service from the operating system, it issues a supervisor call instruction, SVC. This instruction triggers a synchronous exception that causes the processor to switch to EL1 where the kernel handles the request. 3.2 EL1: Operating System Kernel EL1 is where the operating system kernel runs. At this level the kernel has access to the full set of EL1 system registers including the translation table base registers TTBR0_EL1 and TTBR1_EL1, the system control register SCTLR_EL1, and the exception link register ELR_EL1. The kernel configures the MMU, manages page tables, handles exceptions from EL0, and services system calls. It runs at higher privilege than user space but below the hypervisor and secure monitor. 3.3 EL2: Hypervisor EL2 is where a hypervisor runs when the system uses hardware-assisted virtualization. The hypervisor manages multiple virtual machines, each of which typically runs an operating system at EL1. At EL2, the processor has access to the virtualization control registers including HCR_EL2, the Hypervisor Configuration Register, which controls trapping of EL1 operations to EL2. The hypervisor uses this to intercept and emulate operations that would otherwise affect hardware directly. EL2 is optional. On systems that do not use virtualization, EL2 may not be active and execution goes directly between EL1 and EL3. 3.4 EL3: Secure Monitor EL3 is the highest privilege level and is always in the Secure state. It hosts the secure monitor firmware, which is responsible for managing the transition between the Secure and Non-Secure worlds. On platforms using TrustZone, the secure monitor initializes the system, configures the security policy, and handles Secure Monitor Calls (SMC) from lower exception levels. It controls the SCR_EL3 register which among other things determines the security state of EL1 and EL0. #define SCR_EL3_NS (1U << 0) // Non-Secure bit: 1 = EL1/EL0 in Non-Secure state #define SCR_EL3_IRQ (1U << 1) // IRQ routed to EL3 when set #define SCR_EL3_FIQ (1U << 2) // FIQ routed to EL3 when set #define SCR_EL3_RW (1U << 10) // EL1 execution state: 1 = AArch64, 0 = AArch32
Switching Between Exception Levels 4.1 Taking an Exception: Moving to a Higher EL The processor moves to a higher exception level when an exception is taken. Exceptions include synchronous exceptions such as system calls and data aborts, and asynchronous exceptions such as IRQ, FIQ, and SError. When an exception is taken from EL0 to EL1, the processor automatically saves the current program counter into ELR_EL1, saves the current processor state into SPSR_EL1, switches the stack pointer to SP_EL1, and jumps to the vector table entry for that exception type at EL1. The transition is hardware-driven. Software does not explicitly request it. The processor detects the exception condition and performs the level switch as part of the exception entry sequence. 4.2 Returning from an Exception: Moving to a Lower EL The processor returns to a lower exception level using the ERET instruction. ERET restores the program counter from ELR_ELn and restores the processor state from SPSR_ELn, effectively undoing the exception entry. The processor returns to the level and state that were saved when the exception was taken. // minimal exception return sequence at EL1 mrs x0, elr_el1 // read saved return address mrs x1, spsr_el1 //read saved processor state // handle exception eret // restore ELR_EL1 and SPSR_EL1 4.3 Moving to a Lower EL at Boot During boot, firmware starts at EL3. To hand off execution to a lower level, the firmware sets up the exception link register and saved processor state register for the target level, then executes ERET. This causes the processor to drop to the configured level as if returning from an exception. // drop from EL3 to EL1 at boot adr x0, el1_entry msr elr_el3, x0 //set return address to EL1 entry point mov x0, 0x3C5 msr spsr_el3, x0 // set target state: EL1h, AArch64 eret //jump to EL1 Fig 2. Exception level transitions: taking an exception moves up, ERET moves down.
Secure and Non-Secure Worlds 5.1 The Security State ARMv8-A partitions the system into two worlds: the Normal world and the Secure world. The Normal world runs untrusted software such as a general purpose operating system and its applications. The Secure world runs trusted software such as a trusted OS and secure services. EL3 always runs in the Secure state. The security state of EL1 and EL0 is controlled by the NS bit in SCR_EL3. When NS=1, EL1 and EL0 execute in the Non-Secure state. When NS=0, they execute in the Secure state. 5.2 How EL3 Controls the World Switch Transitioning between Normal and Secure worlds always passes through EL3. When Normal world software needs a secure service, it issues an SMC instruction. This triggers an exception that routes to EL3. The secure monitor at EL3 saves the Normal world context, sets NS=0, and returns to a Secure world OS at EL1. When the secure service completes, the secure monitor sets NS=1 and returns control to the Normal world. Fig 3. Secure and Non-Secure worlds separated by EL3 secure monitor.
Registers That Change With Exception Level 6.1 SP_ELn, ELR_ELn, SPSR_ELn Each exception level has its own dedicated registers. This is what makes exception level switching safe and efficient without requiring software to manually save and restore everything. SP_EL0, SP_EL1, SP_EL2, and SP_EL3 are the stack pointers for each level. When the processor takes an exception from EL0 to EL1, it switches to SP_EL1 automatically. The EL0 stack pointer SP_EL0 is preserved and restored on return. ELR_EL1, ELR_EL2, and ELR_EL3 are the exception link registers. When an exception is taken to ELn, the return address is saved into ELR_ELn. The ERET instruction reads this register to return to the interrupted code. SPSR_EL1, SPSR_EL2, and SPSR_EL3 are the saved program status registers. They capture the processor state at the moment the exception was taken, including the exception level, execution state, and condition flags. 6.2 Why Each Level Has Its Own Stack Pointer Having a dedicated stack pointer per exception level is critical for security. If EL0 and EL1 shared a stack pointer, a malicious user space program could corrupt the kernel stack by manipulating the shared pointer before triggering an exception. With separate stack pointers, the kernel's SP_EL1 is completely invisible and inaccessible to EL0 code. /* EL1 exception entry saves general purpose registers on SP_EL1 stack // EL0 stack pointer SP_EL0 is untouched during EL1 handling // on ERET, SP_EL0 is restored and kernel stack SP_EL1 is left intact */
Conclusion ARMv8-A exception levels provide a hardware-enforced privilege hierarchy that partitions software into four distinct layers: user space at EL0, the kernel at EL1, the hypervisor at EL2, and the secure monitor at EL3. Each level has its own stack pointer, exception link register, and saved program status register, making context switches between levels safe and efficient. The Secure and Non-Secure worlds add a second dimension to this hierarchy. EL3 acts as the gatekeeper between the two worlds, and every world switch passes through it. Want to master this? Source: Architectural Analysis of System Contexts, Privilege Transitions, and Context Switching in x86-64 and ARM64 Core Engines
Type: Pasted Text
Architectural Analysis of System Contexts, Privilege Transitions, and Context Switching in x86-64 and ARM64 Core EnginesOperating system environments depend on the hardware architecture to establish security, enforce process isolation, and mediate access to physical hardware.[1, 2] This analysis examines the mechanics of system contexts, privilege transitions, and context switching across x86-64 and ARM64 architectures. It reviews the hardware-software boundary to explain how modern kernels maintain stability and performance during execution state changes.[3, 4, 5]
Foundations of System Contexts and TransitionsA processor context represents the complete execution state of a thread at any given instant.[4, 6] This execution state includes the active virtual address space, the instruction pointer, stack pointers, general-purpose registers, floating-point units, vector registers, and the processor’s privilege state.[4, 7]Operating systems categorize these execution states into three distinct contexts to manage privilege boundaries [8]:User Context: Unprivileged execution where standard user applications run.[8] Access to hardware is restricted, and any attempt to execute privileged instructions triggers a hardware exception.[2, 5, 9]Kernel Task Context: Privileged execution associated with a specific user-space thread.[8] When an application requests kernel services via system calls, execution transitions to this context.[8, 10] This state has full machine privilege, operates on a dedicated kernel stack, and can be suspended by the scheduler.[8]Kernel CPU Context: Privileged execution pinned to a specific CPU core, used to transition execution from one kernel task to another.[8] This context is non-suspendable and operates on a dedicated per-CPU stack.[8]Architectures differentiate between a simple privilege mode transition and a full context switch. A privilege transition (mode switch) is a synchronous or asynchronous event that escalates the processor's privilege level (e.g., from user space to kernel space) while remaining within the execution flow of the active thread.[1, 6, 11]Conversely, a context switch is a software-directed operation managed by the scheduler that suspends the execution of one thread and restores a previously suspended thread.[1, 3] A context switch changes the active virtual memory mappings, flushes or tagged-updates Translation Lookaside Buffers (TLBs), and swaps the register files, making it one of the most resource-intensive operations in system software.[3, 4]
Context Switching Engine: Scheduling and State ManagementThread States and Queue StructuresThe operating system scheduler coordinates task execution by moving threads through a state machine.[12] The core scheduling states are Running, Ready, and Waiting (Blocked).[12]The scheduler manages these states using specialized queue structures.[12, 13] Ready threads are held in one or more Run Queues, which the scheduler queries to select the next task for execution.[12, 13]When a running thread executes a blocking operation, the kernel transitions its state to waiting (e.g., TASK_INTERRUPTIBLE or TASK_UNINTERRUPTIBLE in Linux) and moves its reference to a Wait Queue associated with the blocking resource.[12, 13]Once the resource becomes available, a hardware interrupt triggers the kernel to locate the task on the Wait Queue, update its state to TASK_READY, and move it back to the active Run Queue.[12, 13]Rescheduling Flags and Preemption PointsTo initiate a context switch, the operating system must flag the running thread for rescheduling.[3] This flag, typically represented as TIF_NEED_RESCHED in the thread's info block, is set under specific conditions [3]:Time Slice Expiration: The hardware timer tick interrupt fires, and the scheduler detects that the current thread has exhausted its allocated quantum.[1, 3]Task Wakeup: An asynchronous interrupt handler wakes a higher-priority thread that was blocked on I/O, placing it in the Run Queue.[3, 13]Voluntary Yielding: The active thread explicitly relinquishes control of the CPU via system calls such as sched_yield().[3, 11, 12]The setting of TIF_NEED_RESCHED does not trigger an immediate context switch.[3, 13] Instead, the switch is deferred until the processor reaches a preemption point.[3]Preemption points are safe locations in the code where the kernel can suspend execution.[3] Typical preemption points include returning from an interrupt handler, exiting a system call, or when kernel code explicitly calls the scheduler via schedule().[3]Once a preemption point is reached, the kernel evaluates the flag and invokes __schedule(), which selects the next task from the scheduling hierarchy and executes context_switch().[3]
Involuntary Transitions: Hardware-Driven Interrupts and ExceptionsThe x86-64 Interrupt and Exception PipelineAn involuntary transition occurs when an asynchronous hardware interrupt or a synchronous CPU exception forces the processor to suspend user-space execution and branch to a kernel handler.[1, 7] On x86-64, this hardware-controlled pipeline relies on the Interrupt Descriptor Table (IDT) and the Task State Segment (TSS).[14, 15]When an interrupt vector is triggered, the x86-64 CPU executes a sequence of atomic hardware actions before running any software handler [14, 18]:Descriptor Lookup: The CPU indexes the IDT using the vector number to locate the target Gate Descriptor.[14, 16] This descriptor contains the segment selector and the 64-bit virtual offset of the target handler.[15]Privilege Validation: The processor verifies that the Current Privilege Level (CPL, defined by the lower 2 bits of %cs) is numerically greater than or equal to the Descriptor Privilege Level (DPL) of the interrupt gate.[14, 15, 19] If the CPL is less than the DPL, a General Protection #GP(0) fault is generated.[14, 15, 20]Stack Switch: If the transition requires escalating the privilege level (e.g., from Ring 3 to Ring 0), the processor performs a stack switch.[15] It locates the current Task State Segment (TSS) using the Task Register (TR) and extracts the target kernel stack pointer from the sp0 field of struct x86_hw_tss.[14, 15]State Snapshot Preservation: The hardware switches the stack pointer %rsp to the target kernel stack and pushes a minimal execution snapshot.[7, 14, 15] This snapshot includes the User Stack Segment Selector (SS), the User Stack Pointer (RSP), the processor flags (RFLAGS), the User Code Segment Selector (CS), the User Instruction Pointer (RIP), and an Error Code if applicable.[14, 17]Execution Handoff: The processor clears the Interrupt Flag (IF) in RFLAGS if routed through an interrupt gate, and loads the target handler address into RIP.[14, 16, 17]For critical exceptions where the regular kernel stack might be corrupted (e.g., Double Fault #DF, Machine Check #MC, or Non-Maskable Interrupts NMI), x86-64 implements the Interrupt Stack Table (IST).[14, 15] The IST, located within the TSS, provides seven dedicated stack pointers (ist through ist).[14, 15] If an IDT gate descriptor is configured to use an IST index, the CPU bypasses the standard sp0 field and switches to the specified IST stack, isolating critical exception handling from regular kernel stack execution.[14, 15, 20]The ARM64 Exception Vector PipelineOn ARM64, involuntary transitions utilize the exception model consisting of Exception Levels (EL0 through EL3) and the Vector Base Address Register (VBAR_EL1).[5, 10, 21]When a hardware interrupt (such as an IRQ or FIQ) or a synchronous exception occurs at EL0, the ARM64 core performs the following steps [5, 10]:State Preservation: The CPU copies the active processor state (PSTATE) into the Saved Program Status Register (SPSR_EL1) and saves the return address into the Exception Link Register (ELR_EL1).[5, 22]Privilege Elevation: The execution mode is promoted to EL1.[5, 23] The processor switches its stack pointer register from SP_EL0 to SP_EL1 to protect the kernel's stack boundary.[5, 24]Vector Routing: The CPU queries VBAR_EL1 to locate the base of the kernel exception vector table.[5, 10] The vector table is divided into four main blocks of four entries, with each entry spanning 128 bytes.[5, 10] The core branches to the designated vector offset based on the exception source and the originating execution state.[10] For an exception originating from EL0 executing in 64-bit mode, the core branches to VBAR_EL1 + 0x400.[10]Thread Stacks and Per-CPU StacksOperating systems maintain a separation between the execution stacks used during thread context transitions.[7, 25] Every active thread is allocated a unique kernel stack to process its system calls.[7, 25] However, routing asynchronous hardware interrupts directly onto a thread's kernel stack can lead to stack overflows, especially under nested interrupt conditions.[8, 25]To address this on x86-64, the kernel allocates specialized per-CPU stacks that are independent of any running thread.[8, 25] These include the per-CPU Hardirq Stack (IRQ_STACK_SIZE), used for external hardware interrupts, and the Softirq Stack, used for deferred interrupt execution.[25]When an interrupt fires while executing in kernel space, the low-level assembly handler checks a per-CPU interrupt nest counter.[25] If the nest counter is zero (indicating the CPU is not already processing an interrupt), the handler saves the current %rsp to a scratch register and switches the stack pointer to the per-CPU hardirq stack.[25]If the nest counter is greater than zero, the CPU is already executing on the hardirq stack.[25] The handler increments the nest counter and processes the nested interrupt on the same stack, preserving stack consistency while preventing unbounded growth on the thread's personal kernel stack.[25]
Voluntary Transitions: Cooperative Yielding and Blocking I/OA voluntary transition occurs when a thread yields execution because it must wait for an unavailable resource or an external event.[3, 11, 12] This is common during blocking I/O operations (e.g., reading from a storage device, receiving network packets) or when acquiring contested locks.[3, 11, 12]When a voluntary transition occurs, the kernel executes a coordinated handoff [13]:State Modifications: The system call handler modifies the active task's state from TASK_RUNNING to TASK_INTERRUPTIBLE or TASK_UNINTERRUPTIBLE, depending on whether the sleep state should accept pending signals.[13]Enqueueing: The task's task_struct reference is added to the Wait Queue associated with the I/O device.[12, 13]Yielding: The handler invokes the scheduler function schedule().[3, 13]Rescheduling: The scheduler removes the task from the ready queue, identifies the next runnable task from its ready run queue, and executes a context switch.[12, 13]Once the physical device completes the I/O transfer, it asserts a hardware interrupt line. The interrupt service routine (ISR) processes the hardware event, calls wake_up() on the associated Wait Queue, changes the sleeping task's state back to TASK_READY, and re-inserts the task into the scheduler's Run Queue.[12, 13]On symmetric multiprocessing (SMP) systems, waking up a task is more complex.[13] The scheduler must balance the CPU queues, select an optimal CPU core for the task, and, if the task is assigned to a different core, trigger an Inter-Processor Interrupt (IPI) to signal the target CPU to evaluate its scheduling priorities.[13]
Register State Preservation: Dual-Layer Stack Architectures and SnapshotsTo support task suspension and resumption without corrupting user or kernel states, operating systems utilize a dual-layer register preservation model.[27] This structure separates the register state of the user application from the register state of the task's kernel thread.[27]Layer 1: The Trap Frame (User State Snapshot)The Trap Frame (represented as struct pt_regs in Linux or struct regstate in Chickadee) is placed at the absolute base of the thread’s kernel stack.[8, 17, 27] It stores the exact state of the user-space application registers immediately before the privilege transition occurred.[27]This frame is built during low-level assembly entry.[17] On x86-64, when a system call or interrupt is executed, the hardware-pushed values (SS, RSP, RFLAGS, CS, RIP) are supplemented by software instructions pushing all general-purpose registers (%rdi, %rsi, %rdx, %rcx, %rax, %r8 through %r15, %rbx, and %rbp).[17]This snapshot acts as a save-point.[27] When the kernel completes its execution and prepares to return to user space, it restores the values from this frame back into the physical registers, ensuring the application resumes with its context intact.[27]Layer 2: The Context (Kernel State Snapshot)The Kernel Context is built only when the scheduler decides to switch the processor from running one task to another.[27] While a task is actively running within the kernel (e.g., executing system call code), its active register state is maintained in the CPU's physical register file.[8, 27]However, when __switch_to() is called, the current kernel register values must be preserved.[27] Because this transition is executed via a standard C function call, the compiler’s calling convention dictates that volatile registers are already clobbered.[28, 29]Thus, the assembly helper __switch_to_asm only pushes the non-volatile (callee-saved) registers (%rbx, %rbp, %r12–%r15 on x86-64; x19–x28, fp, sp, pc on ARM64) onto the kernel stack.[28, 30, 31, 32] This set of registers represents the Kernel Context.[27]The Roles of the Process Control Block and Kernel StackOperating systems maintain a clear distinction between the transient execution context stored on the kernel stack and the persistent process state stored in the Process Control Block (PCB).Kernel Stack: Designed for transient, short-term execution storage. It contains active function call frames, local variables, active interrupt handler states, and the dual-layer register snapshots.[7, 8, 27] It is allocated on a per-thread basis to prevent execution state collision.[8]Process Control Block (struct task_struct): A persistent, long-term data structure allocated in kernel memory that defines the overall identity of the process.[7, 27] It contains scheduling parameters (priority, class), file descriptor tables, virtual memory maps (struct mm_struct), network credentials, and, crucially, the saved stack pointer register (RSP/SP) representing the top of the kernel stack when the task is suspended.[7, 13, 27]During a context switch, the kernel transition is executed as follows [13, 27]:The CPU’s physical stack pointer (%rsp) is decremented as the callee-saved registers are pushed, forming the Kernel Context.[27]The final value of %rsp is saved into the outgoing task's task_struct (specifically within the thread description structure).[13, 27]The scheduler retrieves the saved stack pointer value from the incoming task's task_struct.[27]The CPU's physical %rsp is loaded with this new value, effectively switching the active stack to the incoming thread's kernel stack.[13, 27]The incoming task's callee-saved registers are popped from the stack, restoring its kernel context and resuming its execution flow.[27]
System Call Interfaces and Fast Transition MechanismsThe system call interface is the primary mechanism through which an unprivileged application requests services from the kernel.[33] The design of this interface has transitioned from software interrupts to dedicated fast-path hardware instructions to optimize performance.[34]The Legacy Software Interrupt ModelIn legacy 32-bit x86 architectures, system calls were executed using the software interrupt instruction int 0x80.[35, 36] This approach forced the processor to transition through the complete Interrupt Descriptor Table pipeline.[34, 36]When int 0x80 was executed, the CPU performed privilege checks, read the gate descriptor from memory, loaded a new segment selector, and performed a complete context validation before branching to the handler address.[14, 15, 36]This software interrupt mechanism required multiple memory lookups and segment loads, which flushed the instruction pipeline and introduced significant latency.[34, 36]Modern Fast-Path Hardware InstructionsTo address the performance overhead of software interrupts, modern architectures implement dedicated privilege escalation instructions that bypass the legacy descriptor-table verification paths.[34]x86-64 SYSCALL/SYSRET: The syscall instruction bypasses the IDT entirely.[10, 34] It performs an atomic privilege escalation to Ring 0 by utilizing pre-programmed Model-Specific Registers (MSRs).[17, 20]During boot, the kernel writes the entry point of its low-level assembly system call handler (entry_SYSCALL_64) into MSR_LSTAR.[17] It also writes target code and stack segment selectors into MSR_STAR, and programs MSR_SYSCALL_MASK with a bitmask applied to the flags register to disable interrupts on entry.[17, 20]Upon executing syscall, the hardware copies the current instruction pointer (rip) into %rcx, copies the flag register (rflags) into %r11, loads rip from MSR_LSTAR, applies the bitmask to rflags, and escalates the privilege state to Ring 0.[17] The processor does not touch the memory segment tables or modify the stack pointer, leaving stack swapping entirely to software control.[17, 20]ARM64 SVC (Supervisor Call): ARM64 system calls are initiated via the svc #0 instruction.[10] While still routed through the exception vector table located via VBAR_EL1, the hardware transitions are streamlined.[5, 10]The CPU copies the user program counter (pc) into ELR_EL1, copies PSTATE into SPSR_EL1, upgrades the exception level to EL1, and jumps directly to the synchronous exception slot at VBAR_EL1 + 0x400.[5, 10, 24]
Synchronous Handoff and Register Optimization ConventionsStep-by-Step Transition FlowDuring a blocking system call, such as a read request from a storage descriptor, the handoff of control from user space to kernel space proceeds through a strict sequence [13, 17]:Parameter Passing Conventions and OptimizationsTo eliminate the memory latency of pushing and pulling parameters on a stack, modern architectures mandate that system call arguments be passed entirely within the CPU's general-purpose register file.[35, 37]The table below outlines and compares these parameter conventions across architectures and operating systems.The System V ABI for user-space function calls on x86-64 uses the register order %rdi, %rsi, %rdx, %rcx, %r8, and %r9.[30, 40]However, during a hardware syscall instruction, the CPU clobbers the %rcx register to save the user return instruction pointer.[17, 35] To prevent the incoming fourth parameter from being overwritten by the hardware, the system call convention replaces %rcx with %r10.[17, 35]The assembly entry point redirects %r10 to the internal function's standard %rcx parameter slot before invoking the C dispatch handler, maintaining alignment with the compiler's expected register layout.[17, 35]On x86-64, the processor's control flags (rflags) are saved into the %r11 register during a syscall.[17] Upon execution of sysret, %r11 is copied back into the physical rflags register, preserving the user application’s condition codes.[20]On ARM64, the processor's active status flags are represented in the PSTATE register.[22] During an svc #0 instruction, PSTATE is saved into SPSR_EL1.[5, 24]Executing the eret instruction restores the status flags from SPSR_EL1 to PSTATE, preserving the application's arithmetic flags and execution state.[5, 41]
Reduced Privilege Modes and Hardware-Enforced Isolation BarriersHardware Protection Rings and Exception LevelsTo prevent unprivileged code from modifying critical system resources, CPU architectures implement hardware-enforced isolation barriers.[2, 5]On x86-64, the current privilege state is defined by the Current Privilege Level (CPL), which is stored in the least significant 2 bits of the %cs (Code Segment) register.[19, 42]Ring 3 (User Mode): CPL is set to 3.[2, 9] Standard user applications run here.[2, 9] Direct hardware I/O, control register modification, and page table walks are disabled.[2, 42]Ring 0 (Kernel Mode): CPL is set to 0.[2, 42] The operating system kernel runs here, possessing unrestricted access to the entire instruction set and physical address space.[2, 43]Any attempt by Ring 3 code to execute a privileged instruction—such as HLT (halt processor), LIDT (load interrupt descriptor table), or MOV CR (modify control registers like CR0/CR3)—generates a General Protection Fault (#GP), which terminates the offending process.[36, 42]On ARM64, privilege is defined by four Exception Levels (EL0 through EL3).[5, 21] Unprivileged user applications run at EL0, which has restricted access to system registers.[5, 44]If an EL0 application attempts to access a privileged register (e.g., the page table base registers TTBR0_EL1 or TTBR1_EL1), the hardware generates a synchronous exception, forcing a transition to EL1 where the kernel processes the violation.[5, 44]Paging-Level Memory IsolationModern operating systems enforce memory isolation using page-level protection.[2, 45]x86-64 Paging Protection: The page table entries contain a User/Supervisor (U/S) bit (Bit 2).[45] If the U/S bit is cleared (0), the page is marked as a supervisor page, restricting access to code running in Rings 0, 1, or 2.[19, 45]If an application running in Ring 3 attempts to read from, write to, or fetch an instruction from a page with the U/S bit set to 0, the MMU blocks the transaction and generates a Page Fault exception (#PF), loading the violating virtual address into the CR2 control register.[42, 45]ARM64 Translation Tables: ARM64 manages memory translation using the translation table base registers TTBR0_EL1 (for user-space virtual addresses starting with 0x0000) and TTBR1_EL1 (for kernel-space virtual addresses starting with 0xFFFF).[5, 44]Access is controlled by the Access Permission (AP[2:1]) bits in the Translation Table descriptors.[46, 47] These bits define whether a page is read-only or read/write for privileged (EL1) and unprivileged (EL0) modes.[46, 47]The descriptors also implement execution prevention flags [46, 47]:UXN (Unprivileged Execute Never): If set (1), the MMU prevents instruction fetching at EL0.[47, 48]PXN (Privileged Execute Never): If set (1), the MMU prevents the kernel (EL1) from executing instructions residing within that page, neutralizing exploits that attempt to redirect kernel execution to user-space shellcode.[46, 47]Supervisor-Mode Isolation StandardsEven with page table boundaries, kernel security can be compromised if a vulnerability in a system call handler is exploited to read or write user-space memory directly.[49, 50] To mitigate this risk, modern hardware implements supervisor-mode isolation technologies.[49]The table below outlines these modern hardware-enforced protection features.On x86-64, SMEP and SMAP are globally enabled by setting bits 20 and 21 of the CR4 control register.[49, 51] If SMAP is enabled and the kernel attempts to access a user-space page while the Alignment Check (AC) flag in RFLAGS is clear, the CPU immediately triggers a Page Fault.[49, 50]To perform legitimate data copies between user and kernel space (such as inside copy_from_user()), the kernel executes the stac (Set AC Flag) instruction to temporarily disable SMAP.[49, 50] Once the transfer is complete, the kernel executes clac (Clear AC Flag) to re-enable SMAP protection, restoring the isolation boundary.[49, 50]
Advanced Context Control: Floating-Point and Vector UnitsFloating-point and Single Instruction Multiple Data (SIMD) vector registers (such as x86-64 AVX-512 or ARM64 SVE) represent a massive volume of state.[8, 53] Saving and restoring these large register blocks during every context switch introduces substantial memory bus overhead.[53, 54]Lazy FPU Switching and Its VulnerabilityTo mitigate register-saving overhead, operating systems historically implemented Lazy FPU Switching.[53, 55] Under this model, when the scheduler switched from Task A to Task B, it did not save Task A's FPU registers or load Task B's FPU state.[53, 54]Instead, the kernel set the Task Switched (TS) bit in the CR0 control register to 1.[53, 56] If Task B proceeded to run without executing any floating-point or vector math, the FPU state remained untouched, saving execution cycles.[53, 54]If Task B eventually executed an FPU/SIMD instruction, the hardware detected that CR0.TS was set and triggered a Device Not Available (#DNA) exception.[53, 54] The kernel's #DNA handler intercepted this exception, saved Task A's FPU registers to its state-save area, loaded Task B's saved FPU state, cleared CR0.TS, and returned to let Task B execute the instruction.[53, 54]This optimization was deprecated due to speculative execution side-channel vulnerabilities, such as Lazy FPU State Save/Restore (CVE-2018-3665).[54, 55]Because the FPU registers of a descheduled process remained physically loaded in the CPU core during the execution of a new process, modern out-of-order execution engines could speculatively execute floating-point instructions belonging to the new process before the #DNA exception was architecturally resolved.[54, 55]Although the architectural state was rolled back when the #DNA exception fired, the speculative execution of those instructions modified the CPU’s cache state based on the residual FPU values of the previous process.[54, 55]Attackers could construct cache-timing side-channel attacks to extract cryptographic keys or private data across process boundaries without triggering an architectural fault.[54, 55]Eager FPU SwitchingTo resolve this security risk, modern operating systems enforce Eager FPU Switching.[53, 55] Under this model, FPU and SIMD register states are saved and restored on every single context switch, ensuring that no state from a previous task remains in the register file when a new task begins executing.[53, 54]To minimize the performance impact of eager saving, modern x86-64 processors implement hardware-assisted state-tracking instructions, specifically XSAVE, XSAVEOPT, and XRSTOR.[53, 54, 56]XSAVEOPT optimizes execution by maintaining an internal hardware tracking mask (XSTATE_BV) that registers which FPU and vector sub-components have been modified since the last restore operation.[53, 56]When XSAVEOPT is executed during a context switch, the processor bypasses unmodified register banks and writes only the modified register data to the thread’s save area.[53, 54] This hardware-software integration allows eager FPU switching to achieve robust privilege isolation with minimal performance overhead.[53, 54, 55]
Architectural Comparison of Context and Privilege ManagementThe structural differences in context management, transitions, and isolation enforcement between x86-64 and ARM64 highlight the unique design paths of these architectures.
ConclusionsModern hardware and operating systems are co-designed to maintain secure, high-performance privilege boundaries.[2, 7] The shift from legacy software-interrupt-driven systems (such as int 0x80) to highly optimized fast-path instructions (such as syscall and svc) highlights the priority placed on minimizing transition latency.[10, 34]Simultaneously, security requirements have reshaped traditional optimization paradigms, as seen in the transition from lazy to eager FPU switching to mitigate speculative execution vulnerabilities.[53, 55]By offloading privilege validation, memory translation checks, and register-state tracking to dedicated hardware structures (such as page table permission bits, SMAP/SMEP, and PXN/UXN), modern x86-64 and ARM64 processors enable operating system kernels to enforce robust process isolation while maintaining high execution throughput.[45, 46, 49]
